{"parentUuid":"52ebb748-4348-4bf1-95dd-a59e183085e7","isSidechain":true,"promptId":"c0b837de-1474-4940-aea8-21bf4f002cd9","agentId":"a3ca378f920ffd684","type":"user","message":{"role":"user","content":[{"tool_use_id":"toolu_01M5ER6BKJFum5Mp86fyCkei","type":"tool_result","content":[{"type":"text","text":"How to Architect Your CRM for Stronger Win/Loss Analysis | Crayon\n\nClose\n\nSee Crayon in Action  \n\nWe're popping the hood on Crayon's Competitive Enablement platform. Join us on April 1st to see how our AI agents can dramatically improve your compete program [Sign Up](https://www.crayon.co/crayon-in-action-platform-webinar)\n\n[CRAYON, Inc CRAYON, Inc](/)\n\n[Product](https://www.crayon.co/competitive-intelligence-analysis)\n\n[Crayon AI](https://www.crayon.co/product/crayon-ai)\n\n[Aggregate](https://www.crayon.co/product/aggregate)\n\n[Organize](https://www.crayon.co/product/organize)\n\n[Publish](https://www.crayon.co/product/publish)\n\n[Enable](https://www.crayon.co/product/enable)\n\n[Measure](https://www.crayon.co/product/measure)\n\n[Integrations](https://www.crayon.co/integrations)\n\n[Resources](https://www.crayon.co/resources)\n\n[Library](https://www.crayon.co/resources)\n\n[Blog](https://www.crayon.co/blog)\n\n[Case Directory](https://www.crayon.co/partners)\n\n[Get a Demo](https://www.crayon.co/get-a-demo)\n\n[Log In](https://app.crayon.co/login/)\n\nCrayon Competitive Intelligence blog\n\n[Back to all posts](/blog)\n\nHow to Architect Your CRM for Stronger Win/Loss Analysis\n\nPicture of [Tom Heys](https://www.crayon.co/blog/author/tom-heys) on Thu, Mar 28, 2019\n\nReading Time: 7 mins\n\n[](https://twitter.com/intent/tweet?url=https://www.crayon.co/blog/crm-win-loss-analysis\\&text=)\n\n[](https://www.linkedin.com/shareArticle?mini=true\\&url=https://www.crayon.co/blog/crm-win-loss-analysis)\n\n[](https://www.facebook.com/sharer/sharer.php?u=https://www.crayon.co/blog/crm-win-loss-analysis)\n\n[](mailto:info@example.com?\\&subject=\\&cc=\\&bcc=\\&body=https://www.crayon.co/blog/crm-win-loss-analysis%0A)\n\nOnce you’ve decided to tackle [win/loss analysis](/blog/how-to-do-win-loss-analysis-examples-resources) as an organization, the next natural step is to invest some time and thought into how you will go about collecting the data. [Win/loss interviews](/blog/win-loss-interviews) – whether they’re run internally or by expert agencies – are fantastic resources for in-depth understanding of buying decisions. The natural partner for this qualitative information is consistent internal win/loss quantitative data from across your sales team. \n\nBut what data should you be collecting in your Customer Relationship Management (CRM) solution? And when should salespeople be asked to fill them out? Below, I detail some of the fields that you will want to ensure you have in your CRM, as well as some accompanying choices and considerations.\n\nGet your free ebook + template: [The 3 H's of a Successful Win Loss Project](/success/how-to-succeed-with-win-loss-analysis?cid=Organic_Direct_Blog_WinLossEbook_Download).\n\nTable Stakes: The Opportunity Stage Field\n\nSince the stage of an opportunity is so important for accurate pipeline measurement, the existence of this field is near standard across CRMs. At the very minimum, you will need a distinction between Open, Closed Won, and Closed Lost deals in this Opportunity Stage field.\n\nMost organizations will have more than one type of Open opportunity, which can be very helpful in understanding at what point in the sales cycle losses to competitors are occurring. For example, losses in early stages generally suggest the response lies in better qualifying, whereas late-stage losses might drive more tactical needs.\n\nGoing Head-to-Head: The Opportunity Competitor Field\n\nWhile a bit of a no-brainer, it is surprising how many companies aren’t [collecting data](/blog/win-loss-analysis-templates) on which competitors come up in a deal. Of course, lacking any tracking of specific competitors in deals means that you can only perform the broadest analysis of the deals you lost or won. It will be more difficult to distinguish deals that are lost for non-competitive reasons, and your win rates and win/loss ratios will lose significant fidelity.\n\nThere are two primary approaches to field design:\n\nSingle field, multi-picklist: The majority choose this approach, in which the rep chooses all competitors in a deal. The advantage of this field is that it is easier to fill out, increasing the chances that reps will provide accurate data back to you.\n\nPrimary and secondary fields: Some organizations with mature markets or advanced sales processes choose to use a single picklist for the primary competitor in a deal and a multi-picklist for all other competitors. The advantage of this approach is that you can obtain a more exact understanding of head-to-head competitive scenarios. One of the assumptions of this approach is that your business is a “primary” competitor itself in the opportunity; otherwise, measurement becomes very complex.\n\nAfter choosing one of the approaches for storing the data, you should focus some attention on how to fill this empty field. It's important to get the most accurate data you can, considering that some prospects may not be open to discussing the other competitors in a deal with your sales rep. There is also the challenge of forgotten information if you wait too long to ask reps. You should review this implementation with sales leadership to map it to the sales process; in an ideal world, Opportunity Competitor fields would be a required field at the earliest possible stage in the sales process.\n\nGetting to the Point: The Loss Reason Field\n\nLoss Reasons tend to be present in most CRMs by default, and most also have something like a “Lost to Competitor” option in the single picklist. While painful for reps to input, this field is central to your effort to determine competitive losses, calculate win/loss rates, and understand [head-to-head competitive data](/blog/great-competitive-comparison-pages), so you should ensure that the Loss Reasons field is required.\n\nIt is unfortunately common that these reasons are often not customized to your business and market to surface actionable information. Generic options like “bad fit” or “lack of functionality” tend to obscure understanding and demand that you to speak with the sales rep to gain clarity. Why not discuss the type of bad fit that salespeople are encountering and embed those in your standard loss reasons? More refined “bad fit” loss reasons can help you tackle problems across the organization: Sales leadership can improve account targeting and territory segmentation, Product can learn about new capabilities and user needs, and Marketing can refine buyer personas and related [messaging/positioning](/blog/get-out-of-your-head-how-competitive-intelligence-can-help-you-create-messaging-that-resonates).\n\nThe Sad Truth: The Competitor Lost To Field\n\nThis field is overlooked in a surprising number of organizations. Whatever its name, the Competitor Lost To field is usually a single picklist, and the results help you [measure your win rate](/case-studies/upserve) against a specific competitor. You might consider more flexible inputs if you face competition from two or more partners combining their offerings, or if you want to distinguish between different products offered by the same competitor; in these circumstances, a second linked field will provide additional detail.\n\nOf course, many prospects are reluctant to tell their sales reps what company was ultimately selected, so it’s valuable to include an “other” or “unknown” option that can be updated if you ever do learn the winner. You may never have perfect information, but you are likely to have a strong enough sample to drive strategic insight. Over time, your data can and probably will evolve, so it’s helpful to have the mindset that any reporting will be more of a running total than a final product.\n\nGetting Granular: The Competitor Loss Reason Field\n\nWhile the Competitor Lost To is valuable from a statistical point of view, developing a response plan to losses will require more information. Thus the addition of a Competitor Loss Reason can capture whatever repeat reasons you hear from the field. Items like Price and Functionality are common choices to include in a picklist; however, you might find a free-text field more useful since losses are rarely just about the cost or capabilities of your offerings. Like the Opportunity Loss Reason above, the more you can make these reasons reflect what your salespeople experience repeatedly, the more concrete your data set and potential learnings will be.\n\nAny complex human-driven process is bound to have some amount of subjectivity that can color the measurement. Add in the challenge of motivating a [sales team](/sales-enablement) to provide clean, reliable data for which they are neither compensated nor certain about themselves, and any win/loss projects are not simple. Your job then comes down to removing as much obscurity from the picture as you can. Each ray of light that you can shine on wins and losses has the potential to pay back your efforts many times over.\n\n[New call-to-action](https://cta-redirect.hubspot.com/cta/redirect/2355195/70df2e8b-b099-4780-bc79-7072d115fbe3)\n\nPicture of &#x20;\n\n[Tom Heys](https://www.crayon.co/blog/author/tom-heys)\n\nTom Heys, a Crayon alum, leads product strategy at Monitaur, an AI governance software company. His background spans strategic marketing and sales roles in enterprise software startups, high schools (teaching ain't that different), and filmmaking (the real kind of storytelling). When he's not helping Monitaur grow, he will mostly be found chasing his young daughters around climbing gyms, soccer fields, and the house.\n\n[LinkedIn](https://www.linkedin.com/in/heys-tom/)\n\nSeeing is believing! At Eliciting Insights, most of our clients come to us for win/loss analysis when their opportunities are not closing at the rate they expect. While win/loss can answer why deals are not closing, win/loss can also give healthcare vendors a significant advantage in the market. Regular win/ loss analysis can help you refine your strategies, improve your offerings, understand your competition, and ultimately drive your business forward. Win/loss can be performed in house with great success, but there are compelling reasons to outsource it to a third-party research firm that knows healthcare.\n\nThe Value of Regular Win/Loss Analysis\n\nRegular win/loss analysis can offer several benefits:\n\nIDENTIFY STRENGTHS AND WEAKNESSES: There is a lot of noise around why a deal did not close, but by having an honest conversation with wins and losses, you will learn what your business is doing right to win deals and where you might be falling short.\n\nGAIN CUSTOMER INSIGHTS: Get a deeper understanding of customer preferences, needs, and pain points. Perhaps your product needs some new dashboards, or your messaging is slightly off. Sometimes there are small fixes that can be game changing.\n\nGATHER COMPETITIVE INTELLIGENCE: Learn how you stack up against competitors and what differentiates your offerings. You would be amazed by the powerful insights a buyer who recently compared you to your competitors can provide.\n\nIMPROVE STRATEGIES: Use the insights to refine your marketing, sales, and product strategies. If your sales deck is flat or your demo looks dated, wouldn’t you want to know?\n\nOPTIMIZE YOUR SALES PROCESS: Salespeople leave the organization. Sometimes prospects fall through the cracks. We have interviewed “losses” that were waiting on follow up from the vendor. We have also seen scenarios where prospects love the product and the sales team, but the legal process is overly onerous, so they find another vendor.\n\nINCREASE WIN RATES: By addressing the identified issues and capitalizing on your strengths, you can increase your success rate in winning future deals.\n\nWhy Use a Third-Party Research Firm?\n\nAt Eliciting Insights, we have seen many in-house teams attempt win/loss, some with success. Often win/loss gets deprioritized within an organization after a few interviews are completed, and then the team runs out of time to devote to a deep analysis. Beyond getting win/loss completed, working with a partner to do win/loss offers several advantages:\n\nIMPARTIALITY: Third-party firms are unbiased and can provide an objective analysis, free from internal politics or preconceived notions.\n\nHIGHER INTERVIEW RATES: Wins and losses, especially losses, are more likely to conduct an interview with a third party.\n\nCANDID RESPONSES: Respondents are more likely to be honest and open with an external party, leading to more accurate and insightful data.\n\nEXPERTISE AND EXPERIENCE: Specialized firms bring a wealth of experience and expertise, having conducted numerous analyses across various types of healthcare.\n\nCONSISTENCY AND FOCUS: In-house teams may get pulled into other projects, leading to inconsistencies or delays. A third-party firm is dedicated to the task, ensuring timely and thorough analysis.\n\nBROADER PERSPECTIVE: External firms can provide a fresh perspective, identifying issues or opportunities that internal teams might overlook.\n\nACTIONABLE INSIGHTS AND RECOMMENDATIONS: Third-party firms often offer detailed reports with actionable insights, rather than just raw data.\n\nHappy mid aged business woman manager handshaking greeting client in office.\n\nBenefits of Using Eliciting Insights for Win/Loss Analysis\n\nWhen it comes to choosing a third-party firm for your win/loss analysis, Eliciting Insights stands out as a leader in the healthcare space. Here’s why:\n\nexperience\n\nYears of Experience:\n\nEliciting Insights has extensive experience in the healthcare industry, understanding its unique challenges and opportunities. We can drill into healthcare specific topics and provide you with the level of detail you need to optimize your sales process.\n\nproven\n\nProven Track Record:\n\nEliciting Insights has a proven track record of helping healthcare businesses refine their strategies and improve their win rates.\n\nproactive approach\n\nTailored Approach:\n\nEliciting Insights customizes its analysis to meet the specific needs of your business, ensuring relevant and actionable insights.\n\nContact [Eliciting Insights](mailto:info@elicitinginsights.com) today to learn more about our win/loss analysis services [Articles](/resources/articles/)\n\nHow to Implement a Win-Loss Program\n\n[Pragmatic Editorial Team](https://www.pragmaticinstitute.com/resources/author/pragmatic-institute-expert-training-for-data-design-product/ \"Pragmatic Editorial Team\")\n\nwin/loss program\n\n8 minute read  \n\nWin loss analysis and programs enables organizations to understand why sales are won or lost through market research and customer feedback. Learn how to win more sales by starting a win loss program.   \n\n“Why did I lose?” It’s the first question a salesperson wants to ask a prospect after failing to win their business. Unfortunately, the likelihood of getting a straight answer is slim. According to research, prospects share the complete truth only 40 percent of the time in new business situations. This means that in 60 percent of these situations, sales reps do not have a complete and accurate understanding of why they lost. If sales reps don’t know why they won or lost a sale, then the organization’s decision-makers and leaders won’t know either. \n\nThe most efficient and effective solution to this problem is to conduct win loss analysis. Having a win-loss program will provide optimum sales alignment and support and deliver invaluable prospect feedback to an organization. It will help the sales team understand exactly why they lost and what needs to be done to improve their performance and the company’s presentation of its products and services. So, how can an organization get started with win loss analysis and learn how to start winning more sales? \n\nWin-Loss Programs Need Executive Support\n\n \n\nNo amount of win loss analysis will help an organization win more deals if it’s not part of program backed by leadership. It will be difficult to achieve success without the commitment of the president, directors, or other leaders. \n\nWin loss involves, and has an impact on, almost every area of a company. Win loss research will have an impact across sales, marketing, product development, technology, client service, operations, pricing, etc. Therefore, anyone who has direct oversight over any of these areas needs to support the process, starting with executive leadership. Otherwise, nothing will be done with the feedback you gathered.  \n\nFor example, when a large multinational BPO firm sponsored a win-loss program and rolled out the concept to its sales division, the COO clearly communicated the purpose and intended impact on the kickoff call: “We need these results to be more competitive in the marketplace. Everyone needs to pay attention to the feedback as it comes in and use it to improve how we do business.” \n\nThe message was clear and powerful and set a positive tone for the entire program. The company instituted a process where each interview was reviewed by the salesperson and their manager for individual training purposes. In addition, team members from product marketing, competitive intelligence, and pricing set monthly calls to review trends.   \n\nCommit to the Win Loss Process \n\nAnother important factor for success is to position win loss as a long-term process, not a one-time occurrence. Some companies believe they can conduct a single study and extract all the value they need. However, to be truly effective, the idea of win loss must be part of a long-term process. \n\nThink of it as you would a wellness program. Getting to a place of personal wellness requires a committed lifestyle, not a day or week of eating well and living clean. Company health requires a similar effort. \n\nA long-term commitment to win loss analysis and incorporating that information into your processes will deliver results. The president of a large financial institution, who credits continuous win loss research with making him a better leader, said it has “become part of our sales and service culture, and our team relies on the results as part of our drive for continuous improvement.” This ability to review and operationalize data over time will enable organizations to commit to and prioritize their overall wellness.   \n\nHow to Implement a Win-Loss Program \n\nLaunching a successful win loss analysis program requires implementing seven key steps. You can create and deliver the program internally or hire a third-party research partner. In this example, the upcoming steps assume an internal design, launch, and program administration \n\n 1) Identify a Win-Loss Program Coordinator\n\nThe first step is to identify who will run the program. Often it will be someone in sales or marketing. This coordinator will be the go-to person, responsible for working directly with internal stakeholders and sales to gather information and finalize research instruments. \n\nThe coordinator must be someone who can get things done and have enough authority to ensure that different areas of the company will work together. Because a win-loss program will not be successful without buy-in from sales, the coordinator must also feel comfortable working with sales reps to collect and extract deal-flow information. \n\nSome companies prefer to assign a coordinator who has more of an administrative background. However, this could backfire if the sales team opts to ignore the efforts of someone less senior. Try to find someone the sales team already knows and respects. This will ensure that sales data is successfully collected. \n\n 2) Set Up a Kickoff Meeting\n\nOnce the coordinator has been selected, it’s time to set up a program kickoff meeting. It is critical that the head of sales attend to ensure they understand exactly what the program entails. Other attendees should include internal stakeholders who will be involved or impacted by the win-loss program. \n\nAsk each stakeholder to share their objectives for the research and then develop a list of questions to use in the interviews. Also, determine the workflow for capturing information from recently closed won or lost deals, and agree on how to share those results throughout the organization. \n\n3\\) Create and Finalize the Interview Guide\n\n  The best interview guides include input from key influencers within the organization and cover the most important stages of the sales cycle. \n\nThe first step to explore is the vendor selection process. How did the prospect determine which vendors to evaluate, and what were their initial perceptions of the marketplace? Identify the attributes that were most important at the onset of their search. Also, capture open-ended commentary about your company’s strengths and weaknesses and competitor comparisons. \n\nNext, score prospect satisfaction with key sales and product-related attributes, compared with the competition. Finally, conclude by understanding which firm the prospect ultimately selected, the reasons for that choice and why they didn’t select a different competitor. \n\nGet input from all areas of the company and be prepared to create several drafts before your questionnaire is fully customized to your company’s unique needs. Once key stakeholders have signed off on the interview guide, you’re ready to select your interviewers. \n\n4\\) Identify and Train Interviewers\n\nThe next step is to select and train people who will conduct the win loss research. Since it’s important to capture unbiased, honest feedback from prospects, the sales team should not be involved in conducting these interviews. \n\nInterviewers should be comfortable talking on the phone and able to lead informed, probing conversations. They should also have a strong working knowledge of the company’s sales process and product offerings. Offer training to close any knowledge gaps. \n\nIn addition, interviewers should be familiar with hot-button issues that will help them focus the conversation. If ample time is committed to becoming familiar with the questions in the interview guide, interviews will run more smoothly, and interviewers will sound more professional. \n\n 5) Select the Best Deals to Interview\n\n The program coordinator must be able to identify how to acquire and forward the necessary sales information to interviewers. The easiest way to do this is to pull data straight from a CRM database. However, the raw data feed will need thoughtful vetting before it is considered workable. \n\nIt’s important to avoid cherry-picking, so partner with sales leadership to ensure that specific deals are not deliberately excluded. Remember, the quality of an interview will only be as good as the quality of the contact, so make sure the CRM lists prospect decision-makers and their correct contact information. \n\nEstablish a floor for deal size and only request feedback for opportunities above that level. This will filter out the smallest deals, which may not have received the full extent of sales resources to begin with. \n\nIf you can debrief on deals against top competitors, you will gain valuable insights into how rivals position themselves and connect with prospects. Also, capture feedback to help coach underperforming sales reps as well as to learn from the top performers. \n\n  6) Conduct and Disseminate Interviews\n\n  The best time to speak with prospects is approximately two to four weeks after they’ve made their buying decision. During this period, the details of the buying process will still be fresh in their minds, so you get timely, accurate information. \n\nOnce interviews are completed, it is important to share the insights throughout the organization. When you share interviews with key stakeholders, encourage them to share the information with relevant personnel. There is a lot of value in sharing the interviews with others, such as sales managers and individuals involved in the deal. \n\n  7) Aggregate Findings and Present to Senior Management\n\n For win loss to have the most impact, the findings must be aggregated and analyzed. While the verbatim feedback provided in each interview transcript is instrumental in helping individual teams learn from their experiences, the entire company can benefit if it learns from the collective experiences of sales. \n\nAssess your company’s overall strengths and weaknesses and become familiar with what prospects say you do well and what you need to improve. Reinforce data with the open-ended commentary that prospects share during their interviews. In addition, compare qualitative and quantitative ratings to key competitors noted in the interviews. And remember to look at results on a relative basis, monitoring performance and industry trends over time. \n\nTo have the most impact, present these results in-person to an audience that includes key program stakeholders and senior management. \n\nWin loss analysis is an excellent management tool for sales alignment. It helps companies gather prospect perceptions and feedback regarding their sales processes, product offerings, and overall competitiveness. And it allows leadership teams to engage in debate, strategizing in more healthy, productive ways. While win loss analysis allows senior management to focus on solving problems, it only works if the company is disciplined and commits to continuous improvement over time. \n\nAuthor\n\nPragmatic Editorial Team\n\n[Pragmatic Editorial Team](https://www.pragmaticinstitute.com/resources/author/pragmatic-institute-expert-training-for-data-design-product/ \"Pragmatic Editorial Team\")\n\nThe Pragmatic Editorial Team comprises a diverse team of writers, researchers, and subject matter experts. The analysis framework transforms individual buyer  ...    reason their insights feel anecdotal rather than systematic. #  Distribute every Monday. Keep it under one page.\n\nFormat:\n\n```\nWEEKLY WIN-LOSS FLASH — [Date Range] INTERVIEWS COMPLETED: [X] wins, [Y] losses, [Z] no-decision TOP FINDING THIS WEEK:\n[One sentence — the single most important thing that emerged]  ...    ACTIONS NEEDED:\n- [Specific insight] → [Owner] → [Due date]\n``` ### Monthly Insight Report (for cross-functional leadership) Delivered in the first week of each month. Structured around the five driver categories.\n\nSections to include: 1. Executive summary — 3-4 sentences on the most important patterns this month 2. Volume and mix — How many interviews, win/loss split, competitor breakdown 3. Driver distribution — How losses distributed across the five categories vs. prior month 4. Top 3 actionable findings — Each with supporting buyer quotes, estimated revenue impact, and recommended action 5. Competitive update — Any shifts in how buyers perceive specific competitors 6. Trend lines — Are the same themes recurring or resolving? Include a simple month-over-month view 7. Action tracker status — What happened with last month’s recommended actions? (This is critical for accountability) ### Quarterly Strategic Review (for executive team) This is the only deck-format deliverable, and it should be short — 10 slides maximum. | Slide | Content |\n| --- | --- |\n| 1 | Win rate trend by segment (with win-loss program start date marked) | | 2 | Top 5 loss drivers this quarter vs. last quarter | | 3 | Revenue at risk by driver category (estimated deal value lost to each driver) | | 4 | Competitor perception shifts (how buyer perception of top 3 competitors changed) | | 5 | Product gaps costing deals (with estimated revenue impact) | | 6 | Sales execution patterns (common failure modes by deal stage) | | 7 | Champion enablement score (% of losses where champion lacked ammunition) |  ...    | 9 | Recommended investments for next quarter (ranked by estimated revenue recovery) | | 10 | Program health metrics (interview volume, participation rate, insight-to-action rate) | For a deeper look at how to structure programs that actually change sales behavior and not just produce reports, see  ...    | Action taken | What actually happened | New implementation deck created; deployed to team Mar 18 | | Outcome measurement | How you will know if it worked | Track stage 3→4 conversion rate on deals where implementation  ...    AI-moderated platforms. However, the framework becomes significantly more powerful at scale, and scale is where most human-moderated programs hit a ceiling. The operational math is straightforward. A human moderator can conduct 3-4 interviews per day. At that rate, reaching 50 interviews takes 2-3 weeks of dedicated effort just for the conversations, before any analysis. That constrains most programs to quarterly batches, which means insights arrive after the competitive landscape has already shifted\n\n---\n\nTitle: Win/Loss Analysis Template - IdeaPlan\nURL: https://www.ideaplan.io/templates/win-loss-analysis-template\nPublished: 2026-03-04T00:00:00.000Z\nAuthor: Tim Adair\nHighlights:\n30-45 minutes per deal, 2-3 hours for quarterly synthesis # Win/Loss Analysis Template for Product Strategy A structured win/loss analysis template with deal scoring, interview guide, trend tracking, and a filled example for a B2B SaaS platform that lost to a... By Tim Adair• Last updated 2026-03-04\n\n### Win/Loss Analysis Template for Product Strategy  ...    Win/loss analysis is the practice of systematically examining why your company won or lost specific deals. Most product teams rely on anecdotes from sales (\"they wanted a lower price\") or CRM dropdown fields that capture one of five generic reasons. Neither gives you the signal you need to make product or positioning decisions. A structured win/loss program interviews the buyer directly (not just your sales rep), scores the deal across multiple  ...    that run formal win/loss programs improve win rates by 15-30% over two to four quarters, according to Clozd's industry research. This template covers the individual deal analysis (the interview and scoring for a single opportunity) and the quarterly synthesis (aggregating patterns across deals). For broader competitive intelligence, see the Competitive Analysis Template. For understanding how your product is positioned in the market, the [Product  ...    1. Select deals to analyze. Aim for 3-5 wins and 3-5 losses per quarter. Prioritize deals that are representative of  ...    2. Interview the buyer. Reach out 2-4 weeks after the deal closes (win or loss). The buyer's memory is still fresh but  ...    5. Synthesize quarterly. After analyzing 6-10 deals, look for patterns in the factor scores, competitor mentions, and  ...    6. Take action. Connect findings to specific product decisions, positioning updates, or sales training changes.  ...    ---\n\n### Deal Summary\n\nComplete this section after the interview.  ...    Positioning implications. [What should marketing know? Does our messaging miss a key value prop or overstate a capability?] ---\n\n## Quarterly Synthesis Template\n\nAfter analyzing 6-10 deals, aggregate the findings.\n\n### Deal Summary Table | Deal | Outcome | ACV | Segment | Competitor | Primary Reason | Top Factor Gap | | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- |\n| [Deal 1] | Won/Lost | [$X] | [Seg] | [Comp] | [Reason] | [Factor] |  ...    product, sales, or marketing action with an owner and a deadline. ## Key Takeaways - Interview the buyer, not just the sales rep. The buyer's perspective is the ground truth - Score each deal across 10 factors to make analysis structured and comparable across deals - Analyze both wins and losses. Understanding why you win is as important as understanding why you lose - Aggregate patterns quarterly. Individual deals are anecdotes. Patterns across 6-10 deals are strategy inputs - Connect every finding to a specific action with an owner. Analysis without action is a waste of time ---\n\n## About This Template\n\nCreated by: Tim Adair\n\nLast Updated: 3/4/2026\n\nVersion: 1.0.0 License: Free for personal and commercial use\n\n## Frequently Asked Questions How many deals should I analyze per quarter?+ Six to ten deals per quarter (roughly half wins, half losses) is enough to spot patterns without overwhelming the PM or analyst running the program. For companies with high deal volume, sample deals that are representative of your target segment and deal size. Do not cherry-pick the most dramatic wins or the most painful losses. You want a representative  ...    How does win/loss analysis connect to the product roadmap?+ Win/loss patterns are one of the strongest inputs to roadmap prioritization. If integration gaps are the #1 loss reason for three consecutive quarters, that is a strong signal to prioritize integrations over net-new features. Feed win/loss findings into your quarterly planning process alongside customer interview insights, usage data, and strategic objectives. The [product roadmap\n\n---\n\nTitle: The Definitive Win Loss Analysis Template for B2B Teams\nURL: https://federicopresicci.com/resources/win-loss-analysis-template/\nPublished: 2026-01-13T00:00:00.000Z\nAuthor: N/A\nHighlights:\nwith practical insights, frameworks, and tools to elevate my sales  ...    Get the resource\n\n## Win/loss analysis is the missing feedback loop in your GTM  ...    The win/loss analysis template I’m sharing here is designed to give you a strong starting point – regardless of your current maturity level – and help you embed win/loss analysis into your operating rhythm. It’s built on the 9-step win/loss analysis process outlined in my win/loss analysis guide, created in partnership with Glyphic. If you want the full thinking behind the template – and a clearer path to advancing your win/loss maturity over time – I’d strongly recommend reading the guide alongside it. ## What’s included in the win/loss analysis template? This template is a structured analysis framework designed to help you turn messy deal data, buyer feedback, and call insights into a clear set of narratives – and then into decisions and actions. Here’s what you’ll get inside the template: Context & scope framing – A simple structure to define the time window, segments covered, deal volume, and the specific “focus areas” you’re investigating – so stakeholders understand what this analysis is (and isn’t) trying to answer across your sales cycle. Headline outcomes snapshot – A compact way to summarise overall win rate performance (vs baseline or target) and call out the biggest segment-level deltas – the few numbers and headlines leaders actually need upfront. Top stated loss reasons (CI + CRM view) – A section to capture the “raw signals” from CRM coding and conversation intelligence (what buyers/reps say happened) before you interpret anything or jump into narratives, Win drivers vs loss drivers table (lift / drag view) – This table provides a structured method for documenting the call  ...    Root-cause themes + narratives (triangulated view) – A repeatable format to turn patterns into a few clear themes that  ...    Recommended actions + ownership table – A practical activation layer that turns insights into a short list of initiatives, with owners, timing, and success metrics framed as effect size (e.g., win rate lift in a specific slice). The result is a single, repeatable artefact you can use to summarise the most critical findings, guide leadership,  ...    This template is useful anywhere you have B2B deals, a CRM, a CI tool, and a desire to stop making GTM decisions in the  ...    packaging, trust, implementation risk, or messaging – and what to prioritise next in your roadmap. At its core, this template is for teams that want win/loss analysis to power decisions and actions, ultimately driving meaningful sales performance gains. ## How do I use the win/loss analysis template? This template is designed to be used iteratively, not completed once and forgotten. Think of it as a working document that helps you structure insight, align stakeholders, and guide decisions over time. You don’t need to run a “perfect” win/loss programme to get value from it. The template works just as well for a first-pass analysis as it does for a more advanced, recurring motion. You can add, remove, and adapt sections based on  ...    1. Start with a clear slice, not everything – Begin by defining a focused scope in the Context & Scope section – one  ...    Over time, the real value comes from repetition: using the same template consistently so insights compound, questions  ...    ✅ Resources & Templates – Access a curated library of content summaries, frameworks, and templates, plus the Enablement Maturity Blueprint to optimise your function\n\n---\n\nTitle: Win Loss Analysis Template That Actually Improves Decisions\nURL: https://www.onassemble.com/blog/win-loss-analysis-template-that-actually-improves-decisions\nPublished: N/A\nAuthor: N/A\nHighlights:\nsay they do win-loss analysis. What changed since last periodWhy buyers chose us or walked awayWhere competitors consistently beat usWhat decision follows from this If your template does not force those answers, it will drift into commentary.\n\n## A framework that keeps teams honest This five-part structure shows up again and again in teams that actually improve quarter over quarter. ### 1. Lock the context first\n\nDefine the period. Name the owner. State the data sources. CRM notes, interviews, surveys, all tell different truths. Mixing them without clarity creates noise and invites  ...    ### 2. Separate outcomes from explanations\n\nStart with what happened. Wins. Losses. Win rate. Deal size. Sales cycle. Compared to the last period.\n\nOnly then move to why.  ...    ### 5. End with decisions, not observations\n\nInsights without owners are trivia. Every report should end with clear actions across sales, product, pricing, or positioning. Even choosing to do nothing  ...    ## What this looks like when it works\n\nA SaaS team runs its quarterly review. The topline looks fine. Win rate is steady. But losses to one competitor have doubled. The raw reasons look scattered. Price. Security. Incumbent preference. Grouped properly, a pattern snaps into focus. Procurement shows up late. Security documentation shows up even later. The decision is not to cut price. It is to ship a security pack, move it earlier in the sales process, and enable reps  ...    Next quarter, losses to that competitor drop. No product changes required. It tells you where to look and what you must decide. workshops\n\nImprove your win rates\n\n[Speaking](https://cta-redirect.hubspot.com/cta/redirect/2922376/2a02e2ae-517f-42dc-a8db-40ef42615279)\n\nKeynotes & Workshops\n\nHire Anthony to Speak @ Your Event\n\n[Latest from  The Sales Blog](https://www.thesalesblog.com/blog)\n\n[Categories](https://www.thesalesblog.com/blog-categories)\n\nUncovering 9 Closed Lost Reasons + Winning Sales Strategies\n\nAnthony Iannarino\n\nPost by [Anthony Iannarino](https://www.thesalesblog.com/blog/author/anthony-iannarino)\nJanuary 27, 2023\n\nThere is nothing worse than having to mark an opportunity record in [your CRM](https://www.thesalesblog.com/blog/how-to-make-your-crm-a-strategic-advantage) as \"closed lost.\" The second-worst prompt is \"closed lost reason.\" The field in your CRM may have a drop-down menu, so you can easily document the reason you lost the deal. It stings a little to acknowledge that another salesperson won the client's business. It is proof your competitor did something different, something that gave them an edge in creating a preference. \n\nHere will look at nine reasons for marking deals \"closed lost\" and the sales strategies that will turn your experience into future wins. These strategies will help you understand the behavioral changes you can make to win more deals . But before we get started, it's important to know that everything is your \"closed lost\" fault . To make these [successful sales strategies](https://www.thesalesblog.com/blog/elite-sales-strategies) work, you must believe that you, not your client, were responsible for the loss. \n\nHere are the nine common closed lost reasons examples:\n\n[Pricing](#pricing)\n\n[Competition](#competition)\n\n[Lack of budget](#lack-of-budget)\n\n[Long sales cycle](#long-sales-cycle)\n\n[Lack of a decision-maker](#lack-decision-maker)\n\n[Poor follow-up](#poor-follow-up) \n\n[Unresponsive contacts](#poor-follow-up)\n\n[Cheaper solution](#cheaper-solution)\n\n[Lack of internal consensus](#lack-of-consensus)\n\nPricing \n\nYour decision maker told you that your pricing was higher than any of the other offers. They said your price was 8 percent more than the closest competitor. There are several reasons your higher price led to the closed lost status. The first has to do with when you disclosed your pricing, and the second is the failure to justify the higher price. You can use these sales strategies plan as part of your B2B sales process. \n\nSales Strategy 1: When you have a price higher than your competitors, you are better off disclosing that early in the sales conversation. Now you have the whole sales conversation to use the second strategy.\n\nSales Strategy 2: Because you have informed the B2B buyer your price will be about 8 percent higher when compared to your competitors, you can explain how you invest the additional eight pennies to ensure they improve the strategic outcomes they need while pursuing lost customers. \n\nSEE: [Your Higher Price is an Advantage](https://www.thesalesblog.com/blog/the-competitive-advantage-of-your-higher-price)\n\nCompetition\n\nYou are never going to win all the clients you want; you will win all the clients that want you closed lost. In competitive selling, the contest isn't between your company or your competitors. The real contest is between the salespeople competing for the client's initiative. If you lose to a competitor, it means you [didn't create enough value](https://www.thesalesblog.com/blog/what-does-it-means-to-create-value-now) or confidence, and your contact bought from someone who did. \n\nSales Strategy: If you are not already practicing a modern, insight-based sales approach, adopting one will provide you with a competitive advantage. Creating more value and [trust will reverse these losses](https://www.thesalesblog.com/blog/trust-based-relationship-selling-examples) and help you achieve your sales goals.\n\nLack of Budget\n\nIn [The Lost Art of Closing: Winning the 10 Commitments That Drive Sales](http://amzn.to/2hXXgCx), the third commitment is the Commitment to Change. In this stage, you ask your client if they are committed to making a change and if their organization is prepared to invest the money, time, and effort necessary to support it. \n\nSales Strategy: [Never use BANT](https://www.thesalesblog.com/blog/stop-using-bant-sales-method). But continually qualify your client by observing their actions. There is never a reason to go all the way to the point you could mark a deal lost closed without having asked about the money. Ask during a second meeting.\n\nLong Sales Cycle\n\nThere is a saying in sales: Time kills deals. This is inaccurate. Time doesn't kill deals, but no forward progress often ends an opportunity. The most important factor here is your ability to control the process for your potential customer.\n\nSales Strategy: [In Elite Sales Strategies: A Guide to Being One-Up, Creating Value, and Becoming Truly Consultative](https://www.amazon.com/Elite-Sales-Strategies-Creating-Consultative/dp/1119858941), there is a chapter about facilitating the buyer's journey. If you lead the client, you will reduce these losses. \n\nSEE: [Here are eleven ways you give up control of the process](https://www.thesalesblog.com/blog/11-ways-you-give-up-control-of-the-sales-conversation)\n\nLack of a Decision-Maker\n\nIt's difficult to reach your goals when you lose deals in B2B sales. [Clients have their pain points](https://www.thesalesblog.com/blog/how-to-use-customer-pain-points-in-the-sales-conversation), and we have ours. No access to a decision-maker is our pain point. Even though companies prefer to build consensus, you need someone with authority to sign a contract and fund the initiative. \n\nSales Strategy: After a second meeting, a meeting where you created value for your contacts, you can ask, \"Who do we need to be able to move forward, and when should we brief them about where we are now?\" B2B salespeople who ask this question to improve their win rates. \n\nPoor Follow-Up\n\nI don't believe in follow-up. I believe you should never leave a meeting with a client without having another meeting confirmed on their calendar. Otherwise, you will need to buy new tennis shoes to chase your contacts down, as you'll be running hard. \n\nSales Strategy: [Never leave a meeting without another meeting in place](https://www.thesalesblog.com/blog/from-commitment-to-commitment). If this is a problem for you, it means you are not trading enough value that your client can agree to the next meeting. If this is true, you need a new approach. \n\nUnresponsive Contacts\n\nOf all the reasons for closed lost deals, this one is the worst. When contacts stop responding, it is almost certainly because you are no longer being considered. If your sales approach doesn't create enough value for the client, this could be the result. Another reason could also be the fact your client had already picked who they wanted to buy from but had to bring in three companies as part of their buying process. In this case, you were never really in the running and didn’t change anyone’s mind in your meetings. \n\nSales Strategy: You've [lost control of the buyer's journey](https://www.thesalesblog.com/blog/a-better-way-of-thinking-about-the-buyers-journey) and are missing commitments. It is easier to ask questions about how you are doing and if your client is tracking, even if it comes with a tiny bit of conflict. \n\nCheaper Solution\n\nThere are always cheaper solutions available to your clients. A lot of buyers believe the lowest price is the greatest value. Improving your conversion rates will require [a strategy from the One-Up book](https://amzn.to/2ZlYPng): triangulation.\n\nSales Strategy: The short version of this strategy is [teaching the client about the concessions they make](https://www.thesalesblog.com/blog/helping-your-client-choose-one-of-two-concessions) when they underinvest in getting the better results that caused them to explore a new solution or partner. \n\nLack of Internal Consensus\n\nToo few sales organizations have any [sales enablement](https://www.thesalesblog.com/blog/a-sales-enablement-strategy-for-the-sales-conversation) around the often critical outcome that is consensus. Of all the closed lost reasons, this one may not be your fault. When people disagree about change or what they should do to improve their position, their initiative dies, and your reasons for sales opportunity goes with it. \n\nSales Strategy: You can ask if this initiative has support from everyone who will be weighing in on the decision. You can also ask if your contacts will need a unanimous agreement and if someone in leadership is already backing the initiative. \n\nUncovering the nine closed lost reasons examples can help reasons for lost sales opportunities understand why they lose deals and how to adjust their sales strategies. Using the strategies here will improve your win rates, the most important outcome salespeople and sales organizations should pursue.\n\n[New call-to-action](https://cta-redirect.hubspot.com/cta/redirect/2922376/b244ccb3-3105-475e-add2-28052d55bd3e)\n\nTags:\n\n[Sales](https://www.thesalesblog.com/blog/tag/sales) [2023](https://www.thesalesblog.com/blog/tag/2023)\n\nPost by [Anthony Iannarino](https://www.thesalesblog.com/blog/author/anthony-iannarino) on January 27, 2023\n\nWritten and edited by human brains and human hands.\n\nAnthony Iannarino\n\nAnthony Iannarino is an American writer. {"parentUuid":"4f8542c8-9b82-4f37-84ae-755c12e5e588","isSidechain":true,"agentId":"a3ca378f920ffd684","message":{"model":"claude-opus-4-6","id":"msg_01Ki1UY9VZuQ8E7MedrqSQdv","type":"message","role":"assistant","content":[{"type":"text","text":"I now have extensive data across all the requested areas. Let me compile the complete research findings.\n\n---\n\n# Win/Loss Program Design Research Findings\n\n## Complete compilation from web searches and scrapes of actual published sources\n\n---\n\n## 1. WIN/LOSS INTERVIEW TEMPLATES -- Actual Question Lists\n\n### Source: Klue -- \"The 31 Best Win-Loss Questions\"\n**URL**: https://klue.com/blog/31-best-win-loss-questions\n\nKlue organizes questions across 10 stages of the buyer journey:\n\n**The Role (3 questions)**\n1. How did you play a role in the evaluation process?\n2. What are your responsibilities above and beyond evaluations like these?\n3. Tell me about your previous experience with vendors like us.\n\n**Awareness (3 questions)**\n4. What made you realize you needed a vendor partner in this space?\n5. How many voices at your company were asking for a change that led you to evaluate your options?\n6. What were the main issues with your previous vendor partner (if applicable)?\n\n**Evaluation Bias (3 questions)**\n7. What did you think of our company before the evaluation started?\n8. What did you think of [competitor] before the evaluation started?\n9. How have your thoughts about our company, our competitors and the space in general changed over time?\n\n**Requirements (3 questions)**\n10. How did you go about requirements gathering?\n11. How well did [company] align with your requirements?\n12. What were your must-haves?\n\n**Consideration (3 questions)**\n13. Who else did you evaluate in your decision?\n14. Explain the factors that helped narrow down your decision-making.\n15. What were the strengths and weaknesses you saw across all vendors?\n\n**Buying Committee (3 questions)**\n16. Who was responsible for what in the buying process?\n17. Who were the most influential people in the decision-making process?\n18. How do you think the buying process would have been different had there been fewer voices at the table?\n\n**Product Demo (3 questions)**\n19. What was the most memorable part of the demo experience?\n20. How did our demo compare with some of the other finalists?\n21. What would you have liked to see more of?\n\n**Products and Services (3 questions)**\n22. How would you describe our company's product strengths and weaknesses?\n23. How much was customer service emphasized during the evaluation process?\n24. What would make our product better?\n\n**Price (3 questions)**\n25. How did our pricing model compare to our competitors?\n26. How important was price to you and your team in the first place?\n27. To what extent did you feel like our pricing matched the value we offered?\n\n**Sales Process (3 questions)**\n28. What was missing from the sales process and what did you like about it?\n29. How did our sales process differ from others?\n30. How can we make our sales process more effective?\n\nPlus a 31st \"secret\" question at the end of the interview (referenced in their downloadable guide with 50+ questions: https://go.klue.com/guide-for-conducting-competitive-win-loss-interviews).\n\n---\n\n### Source: Clozd -- \"A Framework for Creating the Right Win-Loss Analysis Questions\"\n**URL**: https://www.clozd.com/blog/a-framework-for-creating-the-right-win-loss-analysis-questions\n\nClozd provides 5 design principles plus sample questions organized by topic:\n\n**5 Principles:**\n1. Start with broad, open-ended questions to let the buyer tell their story\n2. Determine core topics and questions to cover in every interview\n3. Formulate additional drill-down questions for specific scenarios\n4. Avoid closed-ended questions (no 1-5 scales)\n5. Adapt on the fly -- skip irrelevant topics, spend extra time on influential areas\n\n**Introductory Questions:**\n- What prompted you to evaluate [category] solutions at this time?\n- How did you first learn about [company]?\n- How did you structure and run your evaluation?\n- What was your final decision?\n- Why was that the right decision for your business?\n\n**Product or Solution:**\n- What specific requirements did the product/solution need to meet?\n- Which capabilities were most important to you?\n- What were your impressions of [company]'s product/solution?\n- What were its most significant strengths? Weaknesses?\n\n**Sales Experience:**\n- What was it like working with [company]'s sales team?\n- What did they do well? Poorly?\n- How did the experience compare to working with other vendors?\n- How did the sales experience impact your final decision?\n\n**Pricing & Packaging:**\n- How did you react to [company]'s initial pricing quote/proposal?\n- How did [company]'s price compare to that of other vendors?\n- Did [company]'s pricing model align with your needs and expectations?\n- What methods did you use to determine the value or potential ROI?\n\n**Competitive Landscape:**\n- What other vendors did you include in your evaluation?\n- In what ways did [company] excel compared to other vendors?\n- In what ways did [company] fall short compared to other vendors?\n- Win: Which vendor offered the strongest alternative?\n- Loss: Which vendor did you choose? Why?\n\n---\n\n### Source: User Intuition -- Full Interview Guide (25-35 minutes)\n**URL**: https://www.userintuition.ai/posts/win-loss-analysis-template/\n\nThis is the most comprehensive template found, with 5 interview sections including \"laddering prompts\" for each question:\n\n**Section 1: Context and Trigger (5 min)**\n- \"Walk me through what was happening in your organization that made you start looking for a solution.\" -> Ladder: \"What specifically about that situation made it urgent enough to act on?\"\n- \"Who else was involved in recognizing this was a problem worth solving?\" -> Ladder: \"What role did they play in shaping what you looked for?\"\n- \"Had you tried to solve this before? What happened?\" -> Ladder: \"What was different this time that made you commit to a formal evaluation?\"\n\n**Section 2: Evaluation Process (10 min)**\n- \"How did you decide which solutions to evaluate?\" -> Ladder: \"What made those criteria the most important ones?\"\n- \"Walk me through how the evaluation unfolded -- what happened first, second, third?\" -> Ladder: \"At what point did your thinking change about what mattered most?\"\n- \"Who had the strongest opinion about which direction to go, and what shaped their view?\" -> Ladder: \"What would have changed their mind?\"\n- \"Were there any concerns that almost stopped the process entirely?\" -> Ladder: \"How were those concerns addressed -- or were they?\"\n- \"What did you learn during the evaluation that surprised you?\" -> Ladder: \"How did that surprise change what you prioritized?\"\n\n**Section 3: Decision Drivers (10 min)**\n- \"When you made the final decision, what was the single most important factor?\" -> Ladder: \"Help me understand why that specific factor outweighed everything else.\"\n- \"What was the second most important factor?\" -> Ladder: \"Was there a moment when that factor almost became the most important one?\"\n- \"What almost made you go the other direction?\" -> Ladder: \"What would have tipped the decision if that concern had been slightly bigger?\"\n- \"If you could change one thing about the option you didn't choose, what would make you reconsider?\" -> Ladder: \"Is that something you felt was a fundamental limitation or something that could change?\"\n\n**Section 4: Internal Dynamics (5 min)**\n- \"How did the internal conversation go when it came time to make a final decision?\" -> Ladder: \"Were there disagreements? What drove them?\"\n- \"What did you need to show or explain to get buy-in from [finance/leadership/IT]?\" -> Ladder: \"Did you feel you had what you needed to make that case effectively?\"\n- \"Was there a moment where you felt the decision could have gone either way?\" -> Ladder: \"What ultimately resolved that uncertainty?\"\n\n**Section 5: Reflection (3 min)**\n- \"Looking back, is there anything you wish you'd known earlier in the process?\" -> Ladder: \"Where would that information have made the biggest difference?\"\n- \"What advice would you give to [company] about how to win more deals like yours?\" -> Ladder: \"What would be the single most impactful change?\"\n\n---\n\n### Other Interview Question Sources Found:\n- **HubSpot**: 15 questions -- https://blog.hubspot.com/sales/questions-to-ask-win-loss-review\n- **Crayon**: 4 must-ask questions -- https://www.crayon.co/blog/win-loss-interviews\n- **Product Marketing Alliance**: Full template (gated) -- https://www.productmarketingalliance.com/win-loss-interview-questions-template-framework/\n- **Genroe**: 13 must-have questions -- https://www.genroe.com/blog/win-loss-analysis-questionnaire/14983\n- **Veloxy**: 15 questions -- https://veloxy.io/15-win-loss-analysis-questions/\n- **SurveyMonkey**: Survey template -- https://www.surveymonkey.com/templates/win-loss-survey-questions-template/\n- **Diffly**: 5 powerful questions -- https://www.diffly.co/blog/5-powerful-questions-to-ask-in-a-win-loss-interview\n- **Win-Loss Agency**: Full question methodology -- https://win-loss.agency/post/win-loss-analysis-interview-questions-you-should-ask-and-why/\n\n---\n\n## 2. CODING TAXONOMIES -- Published Loss Reason Categorization Systems\n\n### Source: User Intuition -- The Five Real Loss Driver Categories (from 10,247 conversations)\n**URL**: https://www.userintuition.ai/posts/win-loss-analysis-template/\n\nBased on analysis of 10,247 post-decision buyer conversations:\n\n| Driver Category | Actual Prevalence | What Buyers Say Instead |\n|---|---|---|\n| Product Gaps / Fit | 23.8% | \"Not the right fit\" or \"didn't meet requirements\" |\n| Sales Execution Issues | 21.3% | Rarely stated directly -- surfaces through champion confidence probing |\n| Competitive Positioning Failures | 11.4% | Almost never stated -- requires 4+ levels of laddering |\n| Timing / Urgency Misalignment | 16.9% | Often misattributed to \"budget constraints\" |\n| Trust / Credibility Concerns | 8.5% | Expressed as vague \"comfort level\" or \"confidence\" language |\n| **Price (actual)** | **~18%** | **But 62.3% of buyers initially cite price** |\n\n**Key finding**: The 44-point gap between stated reasons (62.3% say price) and actual reasons (18.1% actually price) means methodology matters enormously.\n\n---\n\n### Source: The Sales Blog -- 9 Closed Lost Reason Categories\n**URL**: https://www.thesalesblog.com/blog/uncovering-9-closed-lost-reasons-winning-sales-strategies\n\n1. **Pricing** -- Higher than competitors\n2. **Competition** -- Outperformed by a competitor's salesperson\n3. **Lack of Budget** -- Prospect not committed to investing\n4. **Long Sales Cycle** -- Time killed the deal / no forward progress\n5. **Lack of Decision-Maker** -- No access to authority\n6. **Poor Follow-Up** -- No next meeting scheduled\n7. **Unresponsive Contacts** -- Contacts stopped responding\n8. **Cheaper Solution** -- Undercut on price\n9. **Lack of Internal Consensus** -- Stakeholders couldn't agree\n\n---\n\n### Source: SalesHive -- Structured Reason Codes\n**URL**: https://saleshive.com/glossary/closed-lost/\n\nLeading teams capture: **No decision, Timing, Budget, Competition, Product fit, Lost champion**\n\n---\n\n### Source: AskElephant -- Standardized Loss-Reason List\n**URL**: https://www.askelephant.ai/blog/what-is-a-win-loss-analysis\n\nRecommended CRM categories: **Price, Timing, Fit, Competitor, Process failure**\n\n---\n\n### Source: Crayon -- CRM Architecture for Win/Loss\n**URL**: https://www.crayon.co/blog/crm-win-loss-analysis\n\nRecommended CRM fields:\n- **Opportunity Stage** (Open, Closed Won, Closed Lost) -- with sub-stages for pipeline analysis\n- **Opportunity Competitor** (single multi-picklist OR primary + secondary fields)\n- **Loss Reason** (customized picklist, not generic \"bad fit\" -- break down into specific types)\n- **Competitor Lost To** (single picklist with \"other/unknown\" option)\n- **Competitor Loss Reason** (free-text or picklist: Price, Functionality, etc.)\n\nKey insight: Make Loss Reason field REQUIRED at deal close. Customize beyond defaults. Generic \"bad fit\" or \"lack of functionality\" obscures actionable information.\n\n---\n\n### Source: Diffly -- Why CRM Data is Unreliable\n**URL**: https://www.diffly.co/blog/4-reasons-why-you-cant-rely-on-your-crms-win-loss-data\n\n\"A closed-lost reason 'Product' is not telling you if the issue was 'UX/UI', 'Integration', 'Missing features', etc.\" -- CRM reason codes need 2-3 levels of granularity.\n\n---\n\n## 3. PROGRAM DESIGN GUIDES -- Step-by-Step Implementation\n\n### Source: Pragmatic Institute -- \"How to Implement a Win-Loss Program\"\n**URL**: https://www.pragmaticinstitute.com/resources/articles/product/how-to-implement-a-winloss-program/\n\n**7-Step Implementation:**\n1. **Identify a Win-Loss Program Coordinator** -- Someone in sales or marketing with authority, respected by sales team\n2. **Set Up a Kickoff Meeting** -- Head of sales MUST attend; each stakeholder shares objectives\n3. **Create and Finalize the Interview Guide** -- Input from all areas; cover vendor selection process, attribute scoring, competitor comparison\n4. **Identify and Train Interviewers** -- NOT salespeople; comfortable on phone; trained on product knowledge\n5. **Select the Best Deals to Interview** -- Pull from CRM; avoid cherry-picking; establish deal size floor; focus on top competitor deals\n6. **Conduct and Disseminate Interviews** -- Best timing: 2-4 weeks post-decision; share with stakeholders\n7. **Aggregate Findings and Present to Senior Management** -- Assess strengths/weaknesses; compare to competitors; monitor trends over time\n\n**Key principles:**\n- Prospects share the complete truth only 40% of the time in new business situations\n- Position win/loss as a LONG-TERM PROCESS, not a one-time study\n- Executive sponsorship is non-negotiable\n\n---\n\n### Source: Clozd -- Win-Loss Maturity Curve (5 Phases)\n**URL**: https://www.clozd.com/blog/the-win-loss-maturity-curve\n\n**Phase 1: Guesswork & Assumptions** -- General intuition about why you're losing\n**Phase 2: Scouring Internal Data (CRM & Sales)** -- CRM reports; 85% of CRM closed-lost data is completely wrong (Clozd study of 1,000 deals)\n**Phase 3: One-off Research Project** -- Assign someone to conduct buyer interviews; produces eye-opening insights but hard to sustain\n**Phase 4: Ongoing Research with Limited Scope** -- Continuous feedback loop on a subset of pipeline; narrow focus\n**Phase 5: Ongoing Program with Full Pipeline Coverage** -- Third-party vendor; full pipeline; comprehensive data and reporting\n\nClozd's ROI study findings at Phase 5: 71% made changes to sales experience, 42% to pricing & packaging, 40% to product offering, 44% to marketing strategy, 32% to customer journey & churn reduction.\n\n---\n\n### Source: Klue -- 7-Step Guide (from search results)\n**URL**: https://klue.com/blog/win-loss-analysis-guide\n\nKlue's maturity stages:\n- **Stage 1: Sales-Sourced** -- CRM dropdown codes, no formal process, biased data\n- **Stage 2: Siloed** -- One team does interviews but insights don't flow cross-functionally\n- Progresses through to comprehensive, always-on programs\n\n---\n\n### Source: User Intuition -- Minimum Viable Launch (5 days)\n**URL**: https://www.userintuition.ai/posts/win-loss-analysis-template/\n\n- **Day 1**: Pull 20-30 closed deals from last 60 days (mix of wins and losses)\n- **Day 2**: Set up interview guide with laddering prompts\n- **Day 3**: Launch interviews\n- **Day 4-5**: Code responses as they come in\n- **Week 2**: First flash report; identify top 2-3 patterns; route each to a specific owner\n\n---\n\n## 4. REPORTING TEMPLATES -- What the Deliverables Should Look Like\n\n### Source: User Intuition -- Three-Tier Reporting System\n**URL**: https://www.userintuition.ai/posts/win-loss-analysis-template/\n\n**Weekly Flash Report (1 page):**\n```\nWEEKLY WIN-LOSS FLASH -- [Date Range]\nINTERVIEWS COMPLETED: [X] wins, [Y] losses, [Z] no-decision\nTOP FINDING THIS WEEK: [One sentence]\nBUYER QUOTE: \"[Direct quote]\" -- [Role], [Company size/industry], [Win/Loss]\nPATTERN UPDATE:\n- [Theme 1]: [X] mentions this period ([trending up/down/stable])\n- [Theme 2]: [X] mentions this period ([trending up/down/stable])\nACTIONS NEEDED:\n- [Specific insight] -> [Owner] -> [Due date]\n```\n\n**Monthly Insight Report (7 sections):**\n1. Executive summary (3-4 sentences)\n2. Volume and mix (interview count, win/loss split, competitor breakdown)\n3. Driver distribution (5 categories vs. prior month)\n4. Top 3 actionable findings (with buyer quotes, revenue impact, recommended action)\n5. Competitive update (perception shifts)\n6. Trend lines (month-over-month)\n7. Action tracker status (what happened with last month's actions)\n\n**Quarterly Strategic Review (10 slides max):**\n1. Win rate trend by segment (with program start date marked)\n2. Top 5 loss drivers this quarter vs. last quarter\n3. Revenue at risk by driver category\n4. Competitor perception shifts\n5. Product gaps costing deals (with estimated revenue impact)\n6. Sales execution patterns (failure modes by deal stage)\n7. Champion enablement score (% of losses where champion lacked ammunition)\n8. Actions taken this quarter and measured impact\n9. Recommended investments for next quarter (ranked by estimated revenue recovery)\n10. Program health metrics (interview volume, participation rate, insight-to-action rate)\n\n---\n\n### Source: Klue -- Executive Win-Loss Template Sections\n**URL**: https://klue.com/blog/the-win-loss-analysis-template-your-executives-will-love-you-for\n\nMust-include sections:\n- **Competitors** -- Strengths and weaknesses\n- **Sales** -- How sales experience impacted the decision\n- **Pricing** -- Impact of pricing on the decision\n- **Product** -- Product strengths and weaknesses\n- **Selection Criteria** -- Key selection criteria\n- **Business Drivers** -- Primary business drivers\n- **Primary Win/Loss Reasons** -- Why we're winning and losing\n\nFour questions every exec report must answer:\n1. Why are we losing?\n2. Why are we winning?\n3. Who are we playing against?\n4. What are our win-rates?\n\nKey advice: \"Don't throw a thousand things at them. Give them the top three headlines of what you learned.\"\n\n---\n\n### Source: Assemble -- Win-Loss Report Anatomy\n**URL**: https://www.onassemble.com/blog/win-loss-analysis-template-that-actually-improves-decisions\n\nReport must answer four questions without wiggle room:\n1. What changed since last period\n2. Why buyers chose us or walked away\n3. Where competitors consistently beat us\n4. What decision follows from this\n\nReport structure:\n- Report metadata (period, owner, date, data sources)\n- Executive summary (3-4 sentences on what changed and why it matters)\n- Deal outcomes (wins, losses, win rate, deal size, cycle length vs. last period)\n- Reasons won (specific reasons, competitor considered, short notes)\n- Reasons lost (specific reasons, competitor chosen, short notes)\n- Competitive landscape (head-to-head counts, win rates, recurring themes)\n- Recommendations (linked to specific actions with owners)\n\n---\n\n## 5. ROI CALCULATORS -- Measuring Win/Loss Program Impact\n\n### Source: Clozd -- ROI Estimation Framework\n**URL**: https://www.clozd.com/blog/estimating-the-roi-of-your-investment-in-win-loss-analysis\n\n**Three revenue drivers:**\n\n**1. Improving Win Rates (primary):**\n- Formula: Annual Revenue / Current Win Rate = Total Opportunity -> multiply by New Win Rate -> subtract current revenue\n- Example: $10M revenue / 20% win rate = $50M total opportunity. At 22% win rate (10% improvement) = $11M. Incremental = $1M.\n- Gartner found some companies observe up to 50% improvement in win rates\n- Conservative assumption: 10% relative improvement in year one\n\n**2. Decreasing Ramp Time for New Hires:**\n- 25% decrease in average ramp time is not uncommon\n- Example: 10 new hires x $200K quarterly quota x 1 month faster ramp = $500K incremental\n\n**3. Reviving Lost/Delayed Opportunities:**\n- Interviews surface issues that could have been addressed differently\n- Assume 3 revived deals per year x average deal size\n- Example: 3 deals x $50K = $150K\n\n**Total sample ROI: $1.65M incremental revenue per year**\n\n**Online calculator**: https://www.clozd.com/revenue-calculator\n\n---\n\n### Source: Corporate Visions -- Win-Loss KPIs\n**URL**: https://corporatevisions.com/blog/kpis-for-win-loss-analysis-and-voice-of-the-customer-programs/\n\nKey metrics to track:\n- ROI of specific projects initiated from win-loss findings\n- Win rate trend by segment\n- Competitive win rate changes\n- Sales cycle length changes\n- Customer acquisition cost changes\n\n---\n\n### Source: User Intuition -- Program Health Metrics\n**URL**: https://www.userintuition.ai/posts/win-loss-analysis-template/\n\n- **Win rate trend by segment** -- compare segments where actions were taken vs. no intervention\n- **Insight-to-action rate** -- % of routed findings that result in documented action within SLA (target: 80%+)\n- **Time to action** -- average days from insight to action completion\n- **Recurrence rate** -- are the same loss drivers appearing quarter after quarter? (Declining = program is working)\n- **Sales team engagement** -- are reps proactively flagging deals for interviews? (leading indicator of trust)\n\n---\n\n### Source: Challenger -- Revenue Impact Stats\n**URL**: https://challengerinc.com/blog/win-loss-ratio-calculation/\n\n\"Companies that conduct detailed win/loss reporting witness revenue increases of 15-30% and up to 50% improvement in win rates.\"\n\n---\n\n## 6. HEALTHCARE-SPECIFIC WIN/LOSS\n\n### Source: Eliciting Insights -- Win/Loss for Healthcare Vendors\n**URL**: https://elicitinginsights.com/blog/win-loss-analysis/\n\nThis is a dedicated healthcare win/loss research firm. Key points:\n\n**Healthcare-specific benefits of win/loss:**\n- Identify strengths and weaknesses unique to healthcare workflows\n- Gain customer insights on dashboards, messaging, clinical needs\n- Gather competitive intelligence (buyers who recently compared you to competitors)\n- Optimize sales process (legal process, follow-up, demo quality)\n- \"We have interviewed 'losses' that were waiting on follow up from the vendor\"\n- \"Prospects love the product and the sales team, but the legal process is overly onerous, so they find another vendor\"\n\n**Why third-party for healthcare:**\n- Impartiality (free from internal politics)\n- Higher interview rates (losses more willing to talk to third party)\n- Candid responses (especially critical in healthcare vendor relationships)\n- Healthcare-specific expertise to drill into clinical/operational topics\n\n**Contact**: info@elicitinginsights.com -- healthcare-specific win/loss analysis services\n\n---\n\n### Source: AppsRunTheWorld -- Healthcare Software Win/Loss\n**URL**: https://www.appsruntheworld.com/top-10-healthcare-software-vendors-and-market-forecast/\n\n\"Since 2010, our research team has been studying the patterns of Healthcare software purchases, analyzing customer behavior and vendor performance through continuous win/loss analysis.\" -- They track healthcare vendor market share through win/loss data.\n\n---\n\n### Source: KLAS Research (referenced in multiple healthcare sources)\nKLAS (https://klasresearch.com) is the gold standard for healthcare IT vendor evaluation. They conduct ongoing win/loss-style buyer interviews specifically in health IT:\n- Performance scoring of vendors\n- Head-to-head comparisons\n- \"Would you buy again?\" metrics\n- Cited by TechTarget, Gartner, and vendor marketing teams\n\n---\n\n### Source: Porter Research (referenced in Healthcare Informatics 100)\nHealthcare-specific market research firm providing \"quantitative and qualitative knowledge of market opportunities, customer loyalty and experience, win-loss sales analysis, brand awareness\" for healthcare technology vendors.\n\n---\n\n## 7. ACTION TRACKING TEMPLATE\n\n### Source: User Intuition -- Insight-to-Action Log\n**URL**: https://www.userintuition.ai/posts/win-loss-analysis-template/\n\n| Field | Description | Example |\n|---|---|---|\n| Insight ID | Unique identifier | WL-2026-037 |\n| Date identified | When pattern confirmed | 2026-03-04 |\n| Driver category | Which of 5 categories | Sales Execution |\n| Specific finding | What exactly was found | \"Reps consistently fail to address implementation timeline concerns in stage 3 meetings\" |\n| Supporting evidence | Interview count + quotes | 8 of last 22 losses; \"Nobody ever showed us what the first 90 days would look like\" |\n| Routed to | Named individual | VP Sales Enablement |\n| SLA | Response deadline | 2 weeks |\n| Recommended action | Proposed fix | Add implementation timeline walkthrough to stage 3 deck |\n| Action taken | What actually happened | New implementation deck created; deployed Mar 18 |\n| Outcome measurement | How you'll know it worked | Track stage 3->4 conversion rate |\n| Status | Open / In progress / Completed / No action | Completed |\n\n**SLA Guidelines by Function:**\n- Sales Enablement: 1 week response, 2 weeks action\n- Product Marketing: 2 weeks response, 4 weeks action\n- Product Management: 2 weeks response, inclusion in next roadmap review\n- Revenue Operations: 1 week response, 2 weeks action\n- Customer Success: 2 weeks response, 4 weeks action\n\n---\n\n## 8.
